  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_ca592851-f2f5-4735-b76d-a235658fcc8b'
      });
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer13775837683773816875">"One night, after thinking it over for some time, Harold decided to go for a walk in the moonlight."   <span id="freeText13775837683773816875" style="display:none">"One night, after thinking it over for some time, Harold decided to go for a walk in the moonlight." So begins this gentle story that shows just how far your imagination can take you. Armed only with an oversized purple crayon, young Harold draws himself a landscape full of beauty and excitement. But this is no hare-brained, impulsive flight of fantasy. Cherubic, round-headed Harold conducts his adventure with the utmost prudence, letting his imagination run free, but keeping his wits about him all the while. He takes the necessary purple-crayon precautions: drawing landmarks to ensure he won't get lost; sketching a boat when he finds himself in deep water; and creating a purple pie picnic when he feels the first pangs of hunger.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13775837683773816875" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1728797201?book_show_action=true">Aug 15, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ahmad Sharabiani" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ahmad Sharabiani" href="/user/show/614778-ahmad-sharabiani">Ahmad Sharabiani</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1728797201" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer889189482672993398">Harold and the Purple Crayon (Harold #1), Crockett Johnson pseudonym for David Johnson Leisk<br/>Harold and the Purple Crayon is a 1955 children's book by Crockett Johnson. This is Johnson's most popular book. It led to a series of other books, and inspired many adaptations. The protagonist, Harold, is a curious four-year-old boy who, with his purple crayon, has the power to create a world of his own simply by drawing it. Harold wants to go for a walk in the moonlight, but there is no moon, so he dra</span>
  <span id="freeText889189482672993398" style="display:none">Harold and the Purple Crayon (Harold #1), Crockett Johnson pseudonym for David Johnson Leisk<br />Harold and the Purple Crayon is a 1955 children's book by Crockett Johnson. This is Johnson's most popular book. It led to a series of other books, and inspired many adaptations. The protagonist, Harold, is a curious four-year-old boy who, with his purple crayon, has the power to create a world of his own simply by drawing it. Harold wants to go for a walk in the moonlight, but there is no moon, so he draws one. He has nowhere to walk, so he draws a path. He has many adventures looking for his room, and in the end he draws his own house and bed and goes to sleep.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز پانزدهم ماه آگوست سال 2012 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: هارولد و مداد شمعی بنفش کتاب 1؛ نویسنده و تصویرگر: کراکت جانسون؛ مترجم: فاطمه حجوانی؛ تهران، قدیانی، 1390؛ در 64 ص، مصور، فروست: مجموعه قصه های هارولد 1؛ شابک: 9789645367204؛ موضوع: داستانهای خیال انگیز کودکان از نویسندگان ایالات متحده آمریکا - سده 20 م<br /><br />هارولد، پسر چهار ساله، شب هنگام از خانه بیرون میرود، تا قدم بزند. همه جا تاریک است، اما «هارولد» مداد بنفشش را با خودش آورده است. او با مداد بنفش، یک ماه در آسمان، و یک راه بر روی زمین، نقاشی میکند. «هارولد» پیش از اینکه گام بردارد، باید با مداد بنفش، زمانی را که گامش بر آن فرود میآید، نقاشی کند. هنگامی که گرسنه است، با مداد بنفش غذا مهیا میکند. اژدهایی نقاشی میکند، اما از اژدها میترسد. با مداد بنفش، قایق نقاشی میکند و در آب شناور میشود. در تنهایی، نیاز به دوست را احساس میکند...؛ «هارولد»، پسر کنجکاوی ست که با مداد بنفشش، دنیای تازه ای میسازد؛ در دنیایی که ساخته، غول جادوگر را از پای درمیآورد، و باغ را سبز میکند، به آسمان سفر میکند، به قطب شمال میرود، در سیرک ماجرا میآفریند. او داخل تابلو میرود، و دو دنیای خیال و واقعیت را نزدیک به هم میبیند. شما نیز در این مجموعه ی هفت جلدی، همین خیال را تجربه میکنید: «به کودک درونتان اجازه دهید خودش باشد تا پرواز کند.» مجموعه ی هارولد، از کلاسیکهای ادبیات کودک در جهان است، و در سال 1955 میلادی نوشته شده است؛ با اقتباس از همین مجموعه، فیلم، تئاتر، سریال تلویزیونی، انیمیشن و بازی رایانه ای ساخته و پرداخته شده، و در برنامه های درسی آموزش هنر نیز، از آنها سود میبرند. کودکان دیروز و امروز و فردا، و آدم بزرگهایی که کودک درونشان هنوز زنده است، دنیای «هارولد» را دوست میدارند، از آن لذت میبرند، شگفت زده میشوند، و خیالشان بال میگشاید. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="889189482672993398" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer17470621672717974296">It’s amazing what one purple line can do for a story. Harold has a purple crayon and he goes on a journey with his imagination. The illustrations are simple and the book is enduring and cute. Everything is black and white ink drawings with that one purple crayon. It’s pretty brilliant what Crockett did here.<br/><br/>The niece didn’t read this and the nephew is in a phase right now. He’s a bit of a handful and he is just out-of-sorts sometimes.   <span id="freeText17470621672717974296" style="display:none">It’s amazing what one purple line can do for a story. Harold has a purple crayon and he goes on a journey with his imagination. The illustrations are simple and the book is enduring and cute. Everything is black and white ink drawings with that one purple crayon. It’s pretty brilliant what Crockett did here.<br /><br />The niece didn’t read this and the nephew is in a phase right now. He’s a bit of a handful and he is just out-of-sorts sometimes. He had been throwing tamtrums all evening. I thought he a story might turn the tide and it did not. Anyway, he said this was a stupid story and he hated it. He gave it 1 star and told me not to read this kind of story to him anymore. He was wound up tonight. He didn’t want to go to sleep either. Not every night can be a good one. <br /><br />I do think he would enjoy this story at another time, so I might try and read it again. </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer19004833" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer2113218015932399983">I read this many, many times to my twins, and they liked it well enough, but they much preferred <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19543.Where_the_Wild_Things_Are' title='Where the Wild Things Are by Maurice Sendak' rel='nofollow noopener'>Where the Wild Things Are</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/331701.Stone_Soup' title='Stone Soup by Jon J. Muth' rel='nofollow noopener'>Stone Soup</a>. I wasn't sure why, but I never gave it much thought until now. <br/><br/>Now, you see, my little Scoutie Kat loves <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/98573.Harold_and_the_Purple_Crayon' title='Harold and the Purple Crayon by Crockett Johnson' rel='nofollow noopener'>Harold and the Purple Crayon</a>, and I think it is because I finally figured out the voice for reading aloud. One night last month we were sitting around, and I was exhausted, so rather than try to muster energy and liven up proceedings, I simply went with my</span>
  <span id="freeText2113218015932399983" style="display:none">I read this many, many times to my twins, and they liked it well enough, but they much preferred <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19543.Where_the_Wild_Things_Are" title="Where the Wild Things Are by Maurice Sendak" rel="nofollow noopener">Where the Wild Things Are</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/331701.Stone_Soup" title="Stone Soup by Jon J. Muth" rel="nofollow noopener">Stone Soup</a>. I wasn't sure why, but I never gave it much thought until now. <br /><br />Now, you see, my little Scoutie Kat loves <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/98573.Harold_and_the_Purple_Crayon" title="Harold and the Purple Crayon by Crockett Johnson" rel="nofollow noopener">Harold and the Purple Crayon</a>, and I think it is because I finally figured out the voice for reading aloud. One night last month we were sitting around, and I was exhausted, so rather than try to muster energy and liven up proceedings, I simply went with my exhaustion, put in a pseudo-mid-western accent and languidly set off on Harold's journey. <br /><br />Turns out that languid is a big hit -- at least with Scoutie. Slow and steady and soothing is the voice she likes, and I discovered, much to my surprise, that I liked the story more than I ever had before. I think I'd always been reading it wrong, trying to make it into an adventure, rather than a journey to sleep. Silly me. <br /><br />I have it now, though, and I'll never read it another way again. <br /><br />(p.s. Until today I had it ranked at three stars, but I've added another now. Harold deserves it.)</span>

<div id="review_1995634684" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1995634684" />
    <a title="Alex" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3144945-alex"><img alt="Alex" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594856152p2/3144945.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1995634684?book_show_action=true">May 09, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alex" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alex" href="/user/show/3144945-alex">Alex</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">grown-ups</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=baby-books">baby-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3144945-alex?shelf=favorite-reviews">favorite-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1995634684" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5289617217726281060">Here is the first betrayal in a long life of betrayals, child. Your parents told you it was a <i>classic, </i>didn't they? Their eyes were probably limpid with nostalgia as they gave it to you. "It's about <i>imagination,"</i> they simpered. And you took it in your grubby little hands, and you put it in your grubby little mouth, and you thought, "THIS IS BORING." <br/><br/>"But it's about how creativity can take you anywhere!" they cried. And "yeah," you babbled: "Anywhere <i>purple.</i>" Because that's all it is, child, isn'</span>
  <span id="freeText5289617217726281060" style="display:none">Here is the first betrayal in a long life of betrayals, child. Your parents told you it was a <i>classic, </i>didn't they? Their eyes were probably limpid with nostalgia as they gave it to you. "It's about <i>imagination,"</i> they simpered. And you took it in your grubby little hands, and you put it in your grubby little mouth, and you thought, "THIS IS BORING." <br /><br />"But it's about how creativity can take you anywhere!" they cried. And "yeah," you babbled: "Anywhere <i>purple.</i>" Because that's all it is, child, isn't it? It's just purple lines. "No," they protest, "it's pie and dragons!" Scribbly purple pies and dragons, and not the real thing. The real pie is in the refrigerator, the real dragons are in your sofa fort, and this is boring.<br /><br />You might as well know now, child: your parents are boring. Your teachers are boring. You will be assaulted on all sides by books that grown-ups think are good for you, and they will be boring. By the end of it, the love of reading will be crushed out of your body and you will become a lawyer.<br /><br />As you grow, you will face more boring books:<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1312108470' title=" rel="nofollow noopener">Wind in the Willows</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1746285348" title="boring" rel="nofollow noopener">Little House in the Big Woods</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/199093132" title="actually pretty great but not for high school, when you'll be forced to read it" rel="nofollow noopener">Moby-Dick</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/357332886" title="boring" rel="nofollow noopener">Proust</a><br /><br />They are all boring. Did your parents honestly like them? Yes, they did, because they were never children like you. They were born small adults, and they became big adults, and they have always liked boring things. Throw this book behind the changing table where no parent can retrieve it, and read these instead:<br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1976981987" title="DANTON TEN SIX" rel="nofollow noopener">Madeline</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/745990158" title="that's more like it" rel="nofollow noopener">Matilda</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1126522952" title="your classmates suck too" rel="nofollow noopener">Harriet the Spy</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/154863435" title="everyone in this book is crazy" rel="nofollow noopener">Wuthering Heights</a><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/145560209" title="everything in this world is crazy" rel="nofollow noopener">Borges</a><br /><br />And if any grown-up tries to talk to you about Harold and his stupid crayon, bite them.</span>

<div id="review_215536426" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/215536426" />
    <a title="Benjamin Winkler" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6456050-benjamin-winkler"><img alt="Benjamin Winkler" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1317272270p2/6456050.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/215536426?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Benjamin Winkler" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Benjamin Winkler" href="/user/show/6456050-benjamin-winkler">Benjamin Winkler</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer215536426" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1251884941387575847">Crockett Johnson's allegorical retelling of Books 1-6 of Vergil's <i>Aeneid</i> is still as powerful today as when it was originally published in 1955. After being startled by a "dragon" guarding apples - a reference to the Achaian menace brought on by the Golden Apple of Discord - Harold/Aeneas is forced into an involuntary sea voyage, accompanied only by the moon (here a stand-in for his patroness/mother Venus). He lands in a pleasant country, and enjoys a seaside feast (the wealth and luxury of Cart</span>
  <span id="freeText1251884941387575847" style="display:none">Crockett Johnson's allegorical retelling of Books 1-6 of Vergil's <i>Aeneid</i> is still as powerful today as when it was originally published in 1955. After being startled by a "dragon" guarding apples - a reference to the Achaian menace brought on by the Golden Apple of Discord - Harold/Aeneas is forced into an involuntary sea voyage, accompanied only by the moon (here a stand-in for his patroness/mother Venus). He lands in a pleasant country, and enjoys a seaside feast (the wealth and luxury of Carthage), accompanied by a "hungry moose" and a "deserving porcupine", allegories for Dido and her sister Anna. It is no accident that it is Anna who is the "deserving" one - Dido, who spurns Iarbas in favor of trying to divert Aeneas from his divine mission is satirized as thin and rapacious.<br /><br />Abandoning these at their banquet of overabundance, Harold/Aeneas continues his journey. He tries to climb a mountain to help locate his objective (the land where he is destined to settle), but it is only after a plunge into an abyss that he can gain enlightenment. Here Johnson has replaced the Roman conception of the underworld with a more strikingly literal representation - there "wasn't any other side" of the mountain, merely a void. Finally sensing that he is near to his destination, he passes first by a false house and then an entire false city - obvious metaphors for the false destinations such as Sicily which tempt the Trojan settlers. Harold/Aeneas "asks a policeman" (King Latinus) for directions, but, as Aeneas is guided by divine destiny, Latinus merely directs him "the way he was going anyway". Vergil's theme that a divine origin - and the firm religious foundation built upon this - is the wellspring of Roman glory is well symbolized by the ending; the journey of Harold/Aeneas ends when he embraces "pietas" and the destiny placed upon him by Venus, and builds his "room" around her (thus establishing the Roman race).<br />Crockett Johnson's masterful interpretation of this theme through spartan monochrome illustrations offers a remarkable counterpoint to Vergil's ornate Latin verse. But the pinnacle of his creation lies in the titular instrument. It is no great exaggeration to say that Vergil's patron, Augustus, was at the time engaged in remaking Rome in his own image. The author's metaphor is dual - Harold is not merely Aeneas, founder of the Romans. He is Augustus, the fulfillment of the Aeneid's central prophecies, redrawing Rome with an imperial purple crayon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1251884941387575847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText18363523157078668194" style="display:none">This book is Amazing! I love this story of imagination &amp; imagery! My favorite part toward the end:<br />"And then Harold made his bed.<br />He got in it and he drew up the covers."<br />This is so perfect on so many levels. The sad thing is I don't remember reading this as a child - I discovered The Adventures of Harold and the Purple Crayon at age 30 - Where have you been all my life Harold? If you're like me or if you've read it before - do yourself a favor and read this book - rediscover a world of imagination and simplicity. I like Harold and the Purple Crayon better than Where the Wild Things Are, which is a book of similar material, although not quite as simplistic.<br /><br />Some Favorite Parts:<br /><br /><i>He didn't want to get lost in the woods. So he made a very small forest, with just one tree in it.<br />______<br /><br />His hand holding the purple crayon shook. Suddenly he realized what was happening. But by then Harold was over his head in an ocean. He came up thinking fast. And in no time he was climbing aboard a trim little boat. <br />_____<br /><br />He hoped he could see his bedroom window from the top of the mountain. But as he looked down over the other side he slipped - And there wasn't any other side of the mountain. He was falling, in thin air. But luckily, he kept his wits and his purple crayon. <br /><br /></i></span>

<div id="review_1333604377" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1333604377" />
    <a title="Manybooks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks"><img alt="Manybooks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1459773375p2/2970379.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manybooks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manybooks" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks">Manybooks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=imagination">imagination</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=art-and-artists">art-and-artists</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=book-reviews">book-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=childrens-literature">childrens-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=picture-books">picture-books</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1333604377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17713953706212556135">After finally having had a chance to read Crocket Johnson's <u>Harold and the Purple Crayon</u> (which I unfortunately never did get a chance to actually and actively encounter during my own childhood, although I had heard of its existence), and what in my humble opinion makes this timeless classic so very much and inherently, lastingly special is that with the simplicity of the accompanying illustrations, the author/illustrator shows children (no, he actually shows EVERYONE) that imagination and even </span>
  <span id="freeText17713953706212556135" style="display:none">After finally having had a chance to read Crocket Johnson's <u>Harold and the Purple Crayon</u> (which I unfortunately never did get a chance to actually and actively encounter during my own childhood, although I had heard of its existence), and what in my humble opinion makes this timeless classic so very much and inherently, lastingly special is that with the simplicity of the accompanying illustrations, the author/illustrator shows children (no, he actually shows EVERYONE) that imagination and even artistic imagination does NOT need to be realism-based, does not need to be three dimensional, and most certainly does not ever really need to be painterly, as Harold's artistic adventures with his ubiquitous purpose crayon are all depicted as simple line drawings, expressive, evocative, adventurous, but in no way overly involved and with too many minute details (stick figure like renderings that most of us, and even those of us with imagination, and appreciation for art but not really all that much if any talent for drawing, painting and the like could more than likely manage easily enough). <br /><br />And aside from the sweet, engagingly fun act of reading <u>Harold and the Purple Crayon</u> (either as an independent reader or perusing it with a child or a group of children), of showing, of demonstrating how Harold uses his very active imagination, uses his special purple crayon to create his own imaginative world of whimsy, adventure and even delicious treats, one can of course also make use of the very premise of Crocket Johnson's classic to encourage children (either at home or in the classroom) to create their own drawn purple crayon (or whatever coloured crayon) imaginative adventure storyboards, to read and perhaps even to present, to show and tell. Highly recommended and just so much potential fun (as in my opinion <u>Harold and the Purple Crayon</u> simply begs to be imitated, to be used as a blueprint for playtime and whimsical storytelling, but perhaps it might also and equally be worthwhile if not necessary making sure that especially toddlers do not end up initiating Harold too closely by drawing on walls)!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17713953706212556135" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_56022969" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/56022969" />
    <a title="Stephanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1132033-stephanie"><img alt="Stephanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1231396341p2/1132033.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/56022969?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephanie" href="/user/show/1132033-stephanie">Stephanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer56022969" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15055587573171817436">I remember reading <u>Harold and the Purple Crayon</u>, by Crockett Johnson, as a child, so when I saw the book on the library shelves, I decided to check it out for my older daughter. When I read it to her, the feelings of disquietude that had plagued me as a child when I read the book (and which I had forgotten) were reawakened. <u>Harold and the Purple Crayon</u> is upsetting because it is not a journey into the imagination or even into a real yet magical world (a la <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4489.Maurice_Sendak' title='Maurice Sendak' rel='nofollow noopener'>Maurice Sendak</a>'s <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19543.Where_the_Wild_Things_Are' title='Where the Wild Things Are by Maurice Sendak' rel='nofollow noopener'>Where the Wild Things </a></span>
  <span id="freeText15055587573171817436" style="display:none">I remember reading <u>Harold and the Purple Crayon</u>, by Crockett Johnson, as a child, so when I saw the book on the library shelves, I decided to check it out for my older daughter. When I read it to her, the feelings of disquietude that had plagued me as a child when I read the book (and which I had forgotten) were reawakened. <u>Harold and the Purple Crayon</u> is upsetting because it is not a journey into the imagination or even into a real yet magical world (a la <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4489.Maurice_Sendak" title="Maurice Sendak" rel="nofollow noopener">Maurice Sendak</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/19543.Where_the_Wild_Things_Are" title="Where the Wild Things Are by Maurice Sendak" rel="nofollow noopener">Where the Wild Things Are</a> or <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1069006.C_S__Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">C.S. Lewis</a>'s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2451650.El_Leon__LA_Bruja__Y_El_Armario_the_Lion__the_Witch__and_the_Wardrobe" title="El Leon, LA Bruja, Y El Armario/the Lion, the Witch, and the Wardrobe by C.S. Lewis" rel="nofollow noopener">The Lion the Witch and the Wardrobe</a>), from which Harold can return home to his own sweet room and his own sweet bed. Instead, Harold is forced to recreate his own room and his own bed, drawing them with his purple crayon as he drew everything else on his journey. This calls into doubt the seemingly solid world around me and shakes me to my very core. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15055587573171817436" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_733275288" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/733275288" />
    <a title="Scott Rhee" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5717792-scott-rhee"><img alt="Scott Rhee" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1408223108p2/5717792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/733275288?book_show_action=true">Oct 03, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Scott Rhee" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Scott Rhee" href="/user/show/5717792-scott-rhee">Scott Rhee</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5717792-scott-rhee?shelf=dark-fantasy">dark-fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer733275288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17013709561130301185">Crockett Johnson's wonderful little book "Harold and the Purple Crayon" is a classic among children's literature. I have many fond memories of this book (and the other Harold books) about an adorable toddler with an active imagination and a magical crayon that allows him to bring to life anything he draws. I never had a problem with the book as a child, but my cynical (and science fiction/fantasy-addled) adult brain's re-reading uncovers the horrific implications within the story, implications t</span>
  <span id="freeText17013709561130301185" style="display:none">Crockett Johnson's wonderful little book "Harold and the Purple Crayon" is a classic among children's literature. I have many fond memories of this book (and the other Harold books) about an adorable toddler with an active imagination and a magical crayon that allows him to bring to life anything he draws. I never had a problem with the book as a child, but my cynical (and science fiction/fantasy-addled) adult brain's re-reading uncovers the horrific implications within the story, implications that I'm sure Johnson had absolutely no clue he was writing.<br /><br />We start with young Harold, alone and (we are to assume) inexplicably placed in an inter-dimensional plane of nothingness, with nothing but his magic crayon as a link to his home world. This dimension does not even have a moon, so he is forced to draw one. Imagine the utter loneliness and despair he must feel to have to draw a moon, simply to feel some connection and link with the world he has lost.<br /><br />He draws a road, but he soon finds that the road leads nowhere, so he is forced to draw a field with a tree. On a whim, he draws a dragon to guard the tree, but his powers of creativity are so great that his own creation terrifies him. His crayon, trembling from fear in his right hand, creates waves of an ocean, which he nearly drowns in. Quick thinking leads him to draw a boat and eventually an island, and a picnic. This scene is heart-breaking in its innocence. Poor Harold is either unaware of the implications of his plight or he is just so utterly optimistic that he never seems to feel the weight of dread. In this scene we see the inspiring heroism of Harold.<br /><br />I wish I could say that Harold finds his way back home. Alas, he does not, but his optimism and love for life abounds to the end.<br /><br />Who knew "Harold and the Purple Crayon" was such a tragic but ultimately uplifting story about the human power to overcome adversity?</span>
  <a data-text-id="17013709561130301185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3361591613" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3361591613" />
    <a title="La Coccinelle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3972045-la-coccinelle"><img alt="La Coccinelle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1410480686p2/3972045.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3361591613?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="La Coccinelle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="La Coccinelle" href="/user/show/3972045-la-coccinelle">La Coccinelle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25982316-harold-and-the-purple-crayon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3972045-la-coccinelle?shelf=children">children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3361591613" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12319075997872356392">This book has been out for a long time! Despite the fact that it's now 65 years old, it reads as if it could've been written yesterday. Aside from the double length that most picture books of that era had, it's a book that would fit in well in any modern child's library.<br/><br/>Harold decides to go for a walk. Armed with his purple crayon, he draws himself a few adventures, and eventually gets just a little bit lost. The moon, however, has been following him the whole time. With a bit of smart thinking,</span>
  <span id="freeText12319075997872356392" style="display:none">This book has been out for a long time! Despite the fact that it's now 65 years old, it reads as if it could've been written yesterday. Aside from the double length that most picture books of that era had, it's a book that would fit in well in any modern child's library.<br><br>Harold decides to go for a walk. Armed with his purple crayon, he draws himself a few adventures, and eventually gets just a little bit lost. The moon, however, has been following him the whole time. With a bit of smart thinking, Harold figures out how to make his way home again.<br><br>I don't remember ever reading this one, despite its age. I have, however, read Aaron Becker's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1069262781" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>Journey</i>
</a>, which is a wordless picture book in a similar vein featuring children influencing their adventures with a drawing tool. Fans of either of these books will probably find much to enjoy in the other.<br><br>Overall, this was a pleasant surprise. I often find picture books from the mid-20th century a bit long and drawn out. But <i>Harold and the Purple Crayon</i> rolls along at a great clip, and leaves the reader wondering what adventures Harold and his crayon will have next.<br><br><b>Quotable moment:</b><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1590686830i/29556645._SY540_.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="12319075997872356392" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_4494991" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4494991" />
    <a title="Robert Tabb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9600111-robert-tabb"><img alt="Robert Tabb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442473720p2/9600111.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4494991?book_show_action=true">Aug 13, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robert Tabb" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robert Tabb" href="/user/show/9600111-robert-tabb">Robert Tabb</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone with an Imagination</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9600111-robert-tabb?shelf=topten">topten</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4494991" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16293061042485731567">I just love this book (even though my wife insists I never pronounce the word crayon correctly. She says I say "crown"). As an author I try to emulate what Harold does in this book. First, go looking for an adventure. Next, add some obstacles, a little humor, a moose, some pie, and some narrow escapes. Finally, when you're all done, find your way home and get some rest. If I was ever stranded on a desert island, this would be the book I'd want to have with me. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2405707588" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2405707588" />
    <a title="N.N. Light" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23234551-n-n-light"><img alt="N.N. Light" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1483558201p2/23234551.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2405707588?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="N.N. Light" class="user" itemprop="url" name="N.N. Light" href="/user/show/23234551-n-n-light">N.N. Light</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23234551-n-n-light?shelf=children-s-books">children-s-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23234551-n-n-light?shelf=reviews-by-mrs-n">reviews-by-mrs-n</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2405707588" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10369868525983005589">As a little girl, this book was a daily bedtime read. I loved how Harold could create new worlds and adventures with his purple crayon. My mum thinks this started my love affair with all things purple. A great read which promotes imagination and creativity for kids. Inspirational for us grown-ups, too. <br/><br/>My Rating: 5+ stars</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tUE3j08WllrcAAy+JxTWrxZjD//8mNAoBtyVwfg+tQ+nFgtjyNUmTxr0HatabyRK8VweMezmYrveq6fw28RV2g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('x/6acWMrTmDCQ11NaOLbLb4wVYpvStbaN89WbtzGq1XVqaad5Oj+dQS3TFgVmSnIWQ9ERH80ZEnvuGRf/zxLgA==')})
  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4532597777632665967">Crockett Johnson was the pen name of the American cartoonist and children's book illustrator David Johnson Leisk. He is best known for the comic strip <i>Barnaby</i> (1942–1952) and the <i>Harold</i> series of books beginning with <i>Harold and the Purple Crayon</i>. [From Wikipedia.]</span>
